data-av_iconfont='entypo-fontello'></span></span></a></div><div class='entry-content-wrapper clearfix standard-content single'><header class="entry-content-header"><h1 class='post-title entry-title'  itemprop="headline" >	<a href='https://blog.sonicwall.com/en-us/2024/07/disarming-darkgate-a-deep-dive-into-thwarting-the-latest-darkgate-variant/' rel='bookmark' title='Permanent Link: Disarming DarkGate: A Deep Dive into Thwarting the Latest DarkGate Variant'>Disarming DarkGate: A Deep Dive into Thwarting the Latest DarkGate Variant			<span class='post-format-icon minor-meta'></span>	</a></h1><h2><em></em></h2><span class="blog-author minor-meta">By <span class="entry-author-link" ><span class="vcard author"><span class="fn"><a href="https://blog.sonicwall.com/en-us/author/securitynews/" title="Posts by Security News" rel="author">Security News</a></span></span></span></span></br><time class='date-container minor-meta updated' >July 12, 2024</time></header><div class="entry-content"  itemprop="text" ><p>The SonicWall RTDMI ™ engine has recently protected users against the distribution of the “6.6” variant of DarkGate malware by a phishing email campaign containing PDF files as an attachment. DarkGate is an advanced Remote Access Trojan that has been widely active since 2018. The RAT has been marketed as Malware-as-a-Service in underground forums, and threat actors are actively updating its code. The malware supports a wide range of features, including (Anti-VM Anti-AV Delay Execution multi-variant support and process hollowing, etc.), which are controlled by flags in the configuration data. This variant of DarkGate RAT supports more than 65 commands from the Command-and-Control server. The SonicWall threat research team has observed a spike in PDF file attachments that lead to the execution of DarkGate malware on the victim’s machine.</p>
<p><strong>PDF</strong></p>
<p>The PDF file disguises itself as an invoice file dated “26 Jun2024” and contains a download button that redirects to a compromised website to download a malicious VBScript file.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-1-PDF-file-containing-download-link.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-1-PDF-file-containing-download-link.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 1 PDF file containing download link'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 1: PDF file containing download link</em></p>
<p><strong>VBScript</strong></p>
<p>Function names and variable names in VBScript code are obfuscated, and large comments are added to harden the readability of the code. The malware keeps the malicious compressed data in the comments at the end of the VBScript code. The malware retrieves the compressed data using the regular expression “&#8217;\s([0-9A-Fa-f]+)(\r?\n|$)” and extracts files into “C:\Default\Autoit3.exe” and “C:\Default\script.a3x.” The malware executes the compiled AutoIt3 (AU3) script file using the WMIC command “wmic process call create &#8220;cmd /c C:\Default\Autoit3.exe C:\Default\script.a3x&#8221;” which further continues the execution of the malware.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-2-Obfuscated-VBScript-code.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-2-Obfuscated-VBScript-code.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 2 Obfuscated VBScript code'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 2: Obfuscated VBScript code</em></p>
<p><strong>AU3 Script</strong></p>
<p>After decompiling the script file “script.a3x,” we get the legible AU3 script. It concatenates hexadecimal encoded strings of shellcode bytes, which are followed by the DarkGate loader binary bytes.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-3-AU3-script-decryption-logic-and-shellcode.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-3-AU3-script-decryption-logic-and-shellcode.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 3 AU3 script decryption logic and shellcode'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 3: AU3 script decryption logic and shellcode</em></p>
<p>The AU3 script contains encrypted instructions, which are decrypted using a byte XOR operation, and the equivalent C representation of the algorithm is shown below.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-4-C-code-for-AU3-decryption-logic.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-4-C-code-for-AU3-decryption-logic.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 4 C code for AU3 decryption logic'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 4: C code for AU3 decryption logic</em></p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-5-DarkGate-version-initialization.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-5-DarkGate-version-initialization.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 5 DarkGate version initialization'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 5: Encrypted command in the AU3 script</em></p>
<p>After decryption, the below instructions are executed by the AU3 script to transfer control to the shellcode bytes by registering a callback using API EnumWindows.</p>
<p>DllStructCreate(&#8220;byte[75613]&#8221;) DllCall(&#8220;kernel32.dll&#8221;, &#8220;BOOL&#8221;, &#8220;VirtualProtect&#8221;, &#8220;ptr&#8221;, DllStructGetPtr($pt), &#8220;int&#8221;, 75613, &#8220;dword&#8221;, 0x40, &#8220;dword*&#8221;, &#8220;null) DllStructSetData($pt, 1, $data) DllCall(&#8220;user32.dll&#8221;, &#8220;int&#8221;, &#8220;EnumWindows&#8221;, &#8220;ptr&#8221;, DllStructGetPtr($pt), &#8220;lparam&#8221;, 0)</p>
<p><strong>Shellcode</strong></p>
<p>The shellcode does PEB traversal to resolve API addresses using API names hashing. The shellcode enumerates PE headers of the DarkGate loader binary, which is followed by the shellcode bytes to get the address of the entry point. Execution is now transferred to the DarkGate loader entry point, and the 0x20th byte in the DOS header of the loader binary is updated to the value “2” to prevent re-execution of the loader binary in the next callback to the shellcode. The shellcode is registered as a callback function, and the updated value of the 0x20th byte in the DOS header helps to prevent multi-instances execution for the DarkGate loader.</p>
<p><strong>DarkGate Loader</strong></p>
<p>The loader reads the script file and retrieves the encrypted DarkGate bytes using the marker value “GDrdcpJy.” The malware decrypts the DarkGate binary with the key “GDrdcpJy” using the EncryptDecrypt algorithm, which is explained in the malware initialization section. The malware now loads the DarkGate binary in memory and transfers execution control to it.</p>
<p><strong>DarkGate</strong></p>
<p>Malware execution starts with initializing the version value “6.6” for the DarkGate variant. It loads the required DLLs and resolves APIs addresses dynamically at runtime in later stages to harden the analysis. Below is the list of loaded DLLs by the malware.</p>
<ul>
<li>Urlmon.dll</li>
<li>user32.dll</li>
<li>Advapi32.dll</li>
<li>Shell32.dll</li>
<li>ntdll.dll</li>
</ul>
<p>The malware invokes a module which is responsible for the initialization of the key value. This key is used by the malware to encrypt and decrypt data.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-6-Encrypted-command-in-AU3-script.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-6-Encrypted-command-in-AU3-script.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 6 Encrypted command in AU3 script'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p>Figure 6: DarkGate version initialization</p>
<p><strong>Key Initialization</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Gets value for “ProductID” from registry entry HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\CurrentVersion.</li>
<li>Gets value for “ProcessorNameString” from registry entry HKLM\HARDWARE\DESCRIPTION\System\CentralProcessor.</li>
<li>Gets hexadecimal encoded Unicode computer name using the API GetComputerNameW.</li>
<li>Concatenates values (string “4” + ProductID + ProcessorInfo + ComputerName).</li>
<li>Generates a customized MD5 value from the concatenated string.</li>
<li>Computes MD5 from the customized MD5 value and performs substitute cipher encoding using the cipher table “abcdefKhABCDEFGH” to get the encoded string.</li>
<li>Takes the initial 7 bytes “hbKEHBK” from the encoded string to create a file in %appdata%. If the %appdata% directory is not present on the machine, then the malware creates a file in the “c:\temp” directory.</li>
<li>Generates a random string of length 0x14 and computes its MD5 and performs substitute cipher encoding to get the encoded string.</li>
<li>The encoded string “GehdKEDaHaDcEbEeDHKAdeKFGDDdAhAd” is written into the file “%appdata%\ hbKEHBK.” The value is read from the file in every next execution of the malware on the same machine to compute the key value, which is used to encrypt and decrypt data.</li>
<li>Reads file content from “%appdata%\ hbKEHBK” and generates a customized MD5 value, then gets the key value by computing MD5 and encoding using the substitute cipher method from the customized MD5 value.</li>
<li>Saves the key value “fHFeFhhCEhbBKBcfKEAbCBeHFCHFEhFK” into memory.</li>
</ul>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-7-Key-generation-process.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-7-Key-generation-process.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 7 Key generation process'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 7: Key generation process</em></p>
<p>Whenever we refer to the key to encrypt and decrypt data, we will be referring to this key value saved in the memory. Values mentioned above are specific to the infected system and vary on different systems. These values are mentioned for better understanding and referencing purposes.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-8-Code-snippet-to-get-initial-7-characters-for-appdata-file-name.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-8-Code-snippet-to-get-initial-7-characters-for-appdata-file-name.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 8 Code snippet to get initial 7 characters for appdata file name'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 8: Code snippet to get the initial 7 characters for the appdata file name</em></p>
<p><strong>Test Environment Settings</strong></p>
<p>The malware author has implemented a file-based detection method to detect a testing environment to avoid debugging and modification of the code while testing the malware execution. Malware execution can be disabled by creating a file “&#99;&#58;&#92;temp&#92;test&#46;txt” which forces the malware to terminate after creating a file “&#99;&#58;&#92;temp&#92;test_ok.” The presence of “&#99;&#58;&#92;temp&#92;test&#46;txt” on the machine can also save users from DarkGate infection.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-9-Checks-for-testing-environment.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-9-Checks-for-testing-environment.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 9 Checks for testing environment'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 9: Checks for testing environment</em></p>
<p><strong>AntiVirus Detection</strong></p>
<p>The malware enumerates the processes and saves the list of process names separated by “|.” It detects the security software based on either the presence of the installation directory or by the presence of the process name related to the security software. If security software is detected, the malware sets the corresponding flag and initializes the name string for that security software. If the malware does not find any security software, then it considers the presence of Windows Defender and initializes the flag and name values accordingly. Flag values are used to alter malware behavior based on the presence of particular security software. A list of security software and their detection methods are mentioned in the below table.</p>
<table>
<tbody>
<tr>

</tr>
<tr>

<p>To prevent false detection of security processes for smaller process names, the malware uses “|” with the process name while searching in the list of running processes. As “|” is used as a separator in the running process names list, it will avoid any match from the middle of the running process name.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-11-Code-snippet-comparing-installation-directory-for-security-software-1030x592.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-11-Code-snippet-comparing-installation-directory-for-security-software.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 11 Code snippet comparing installation directory for security software'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 11: Code snippet comparing installation directory for security software</em></p>
<p><strong>Malware Initialization</strong></p>
<p>The malware decrypts the configuration data from memory with the key “ckcilIcconnh” using an XOR-based algorithm. The malware uses the same algorithm to encrypt and decrypt data but with a different key. We will be referring to this algorithm as the EncryptDecrypt algorithm in further discussion.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-12-EncryptDecrypt-algorithm.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-12-EncryptDecrypt-algorithm.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 12 EncryptDecrypt algorithm'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 12: EncryptDecrypt algorithm</em></p>
<p>The decrypted data is a representation of key-value pairs, where keys are integer indexes and values are either “Yes” or “No” flags or can be data used by the malware.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-13-Decrypted-configuration-data.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-13-Decrypted-configuration-data.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 13 Decrypted configuration data'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 13: Decrypted configuration data</em></p>
<p>The malware generates hash-encrypted folder names from corresponding plain text folder names.</p>
<table>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>Plain text folder name</td>
<td>Hashed folder name</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Mainfolder</td>
<td>Dehffdh</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Logsfolder</td>
<td>Chhdddd</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Settings</td>
<td>Ddahcgk</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Domain</td>
<td>Kkgfbgh</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p><em>Figure 14: Hash-based folder names</em></p>
<p>The malware creates the hash-encrypted named mainfolder “dehffdh” in “C:\ProgramData.” Instead of “C:\ProgramData,” the malware uses the directory “C:\” if any of the Avast or AVG security software is present on the victim’s machine. The malware creates other folders and files in the mainfolder.</p>
<ul>
<li>C:\ProgramData\dehffdh\</li>
<li>C:\ProgramData\dehffdh\chhdddd\</li>
<li>C:\ProgramData\dehffdh\ddahcgk</li>
<li>C:\ProgramData\dehffdh\kkgfbgh</li>
</ul>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-15-Code-gets-hash-based-names-from-plain-folder-and-file-names.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-15-Code-gets-hash-based-names-from-plain-folder-and-file-names.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 15 Code gets hash-based names from plain folder and file names'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 15: Code gets hash-based names from plain folder and file names</em></p>
<p>The code appends “Domain&#61;&#60;host IP&#62;” and “EPOCH&#61;&#60;current timestamp&#62;” to the configuration data, encrypts it using a stored key with the EncryptDecrypt algorithm, and writes the encrypted data into the settings file located at “&#67;&#58;&#92;ProgramData&#92;dehffdh&#92;ddahcgk.” Additionally, it captures domain information using the command “cmd&#46;exe &#47;&#99;z wmic ComputerSystem get domain” and stores it in the domain file “&#67;&#58;&#92;ProgramData&#92;dehffdh&#92;kkgfbgh.” The code also conceals the main folder if Avast or AVG security software is detected on the machine.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-16-Gets-domain-information.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-16-Gets-domain-information.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 16 Gets domain information'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p>Figure 16: Gets domain information.</p>
<p><strong>Debug Mode Network Communication </strong></p>
<p>If the malware finds the string “optpad” in the encrypted configuration data, it considers the execution in a debug environment and uses localhost (127.0.0.1) instead of the actual Command and Control (C2) host. This might be done by the malware author to investigate the proper working of the network communication with the malware. The malware also checks for the presence of the directory “c:\debug” to display a debug message with the DarkGate version number.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-17-Debug-message-with-DarkGate-version.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-17-Debug-message-with-DarkGate-version.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 17 Debug message with DarkGate version'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p>Figure 17: Debug message with DarkGate version</p>

<p><strong>Anti-VM</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>If flag 5 is &#8220;Yes,&#8221; the malware gets the value for &#8220;ProcessorNameString&#8221; from registry entry HKLM\HARDWARE\DESCRIPTION\System\CentralProcessor and checks for the string &#8220;xeon&#8221; to terminate malware execution.</li>
<li>If flag 3 is &#8220;Yes&#8221; or flag 6 is &#8220;Yes,&#8221; the malware checks for the following strings in the display device name to terminate malware execution:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>microsoft hyper-v video</li>
<li>virtual</li>
<li>vmware</li>
<li>standard vga graphics adapter</li>
<li>microsoft basic display adapter</li>
</ul>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-19-Compares-display-device-name.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-19-Compares-display-device-name.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 19 Compares display device name'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 19: Compares display device name</em></p>
<ol start="3">
<li>If flag 7 is &#8220;Yes,&#8221; the malware retrieves the minimum RAM size value &#8220;4095&#8221; from field data 19, and if the system RAM size in MB is less than the minimum RAM size required, the malware terminates its execution.</li>
<li>If flag 4 is &#8220;Yes,&#8221; the malware retrieves the minimum hard disk size value &#8220;100&#8221; from data 18, and if the system hard disk size in GB is less than the minimum hard disk size required, the malware terminates its execution.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Multi Variant Support</strong></p>
<p>The malware code is written to support three types of variants listed below and behaves accordingly:</p>
<ul>
<li>AutoHotkey variant (flag 31)</li>
<li>AutoIt v3 variant (flag 23)</li>
<li>DLL variant (flag 22)</li>
</ul>
<p>This malware variant is an AutoIt V3 (AU3) variant, which is identified by the value &#8220;Yes&#8221; for flag 31.</p>
<p><strong>Actions Based on Installed AV</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>If any security software from nod32 (ESET), Avast, or AVG is present on the victim&#8217;s machine, the malware sets the value for flag 1 and flag 32 to &#8220;Yes,&#8221; enabling the execution of the malware using process hollowing.</li>
<li>If ESET is present, the malware checks for the username &#8220;abby&#8221; to terminate the malware execution.</li>
<li>If SentinalOne or Bitdefender is present, the malware displays a message box containing random text of length 6 using API MessageBoxTimeoutA. However, the message cannot be seen by the user as it has a timeout value of only 2 milliseconds and disappears immediately.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Delay Execution</strong></p>
<p>Malware delays execution for some time if the user is focused on the Process Hacker or Process Monitor window, to avoid malicious activity observation from the user. The malware runs in a 100 milliseconds sleep loop for 40 times in which foreground window text is checked for strings &#8220;process hacker&#8221; or &#8220;process explorer,&#8221; and if it does not match, the malware exits from the loop.</p>
<p><strong>Hello Message</strong></p>
<p>If the value of flag 8 is &#8220;Yes,&#8221; the malware takes the value &#8220;DarkGate&#8221; from field data 8 as caption and decrypts the value from field data 12 using custom Base64 decoding to use as text for displaying in the message box with a timeout value of 1770 milliseconds.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-20-DarkGate-says-Hello-World.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-20-DarkGate-says-Hello-World.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 20 DarkGate says Hello World!'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 20: DarkGate says Hello World!</em></p>
<p><strong>Malware Installation</strong></p>
<p>If the value of flag 1 is &#8220;yes,&#8221; the malware retrieves the running executable path and script path from the process arguments to copy them into Autoit3.exe and AU3 script respectively into the main folder in &#8220;c:\ProgramData&#8221;. The malware decrypts the DarkGate binary with the key value &#8220;GDrdcpJy&#8221; from field data 27 using the EncryptDecrypt algorithm. The key value also works as a marker to retrieve the encrypted DarkGate bytes from the AU3 script file.</p>
<p><strong>Process Hollowing</strong></p>
<p>If the value of flag 1 and flag 32 is &#8220;Yes,&#8221; the malware invokes the process hollowing code. If Norton security software is found, the malware finds the process name &#8220;Norton.exe&#8221; in running processes to load and inject the DarkGate binary. If SentinalOne is present on the victim&#8217;s machine, the malware skips process hollowing. If SentinalOne is not present, the malware targets the following files sequentially for process hollowing:</p>
<ul>
<li>C:\Program Files (x86)\Microsoft\EdgeUpdate\substring&lt;updatecore.exe&gt;</li>
<li>C:\Program Files (x86)\Microsoft\EdgeUpdate\MicrosoftEdgeUpdate.exe</li>
<li>C:\Program Files (x86)\Google\Update\substring&lt;updatecore.exe&gt;</li>
<li>C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework\v4.0.30319\msbuild.exe</li>
</ul>
<p>If the value of flag 1 is &#8220;Yes&#8221; and the value of flag 32 is &#8220;No,&#8221; the malware skips process hollowing and creates a persistence entry by dropping a Windows Shortcut (LNK) file into &#8220;%appdata%\Microsoft\Windows\Start Menu\Programs\Startup&#8221;. The LNK file launches the AU3 script using Autoit3.exe on Windows startup, which further executes the DarkGate malware from the script file. The malware spawns a thread that keeps looking for foreground windows text and deletes the dropped LNK file if it finds one of the following strings:</p>
<ul>
<li>process hacker</li>
<li>process explorer</li>
<li>ccleaner</li>
<li>system config</li>
<li>malwarebytes</li>
<li>farbar recovery</li>
<li>avast</li>
<li>startup</li>
<li>rootkit</li>
<li>autoruns</li>
<li>editor de registro</li>
<li>editor del registro</li>
<li>registry editor</li>
<li>gerenciador de tarefas</li>
<li>zhpcleaner</li>
<li>task manager</li>
<li>junkware removal</li>
<li>administrador de tareas</li>
<li>hijackthis</li>
<li>tcpview</li>
<li>process monitor</li>
<li>wireshark</li>
<li>taskmanager</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Prevent Sleeps</strong></p>
<p>Before starting communication with the C2 server, the malware calls API SetThreadExecutionState to prevent the system from sleeping.</p>
<p><strong>Network Communication</strong></p>
<p>The malware collects the following information from the victim&#8217;s machine and concatenates them using separator &#8220;|&#8221;:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hexadecimal encoded Unicode text from active window</li>
<li>Last input time</li>
<li>Time in seconds from system start</li>
<li>Is user admin</li>
<li>DarkGate version</li>
</ul>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-21-Information-sent-to-C2-in-plain-text.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-21-Information-sent-to-C2-in-plain-text.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 21 Information sent to C2 in plain text'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 21: Information sent to C2 in plain text</em></p>
<p>The data is concatenated with the value &#8220;1000&#8221; and then encrypted using the EncryptDecrypt algorithm mentioned earlier with the key saved in memory. The malware concatenates the key and encrypted data and encodes using custom Base64 encoding. The malware sends the encoded data to the C2 server &#8220;91.222.173.170&#8221;. If the value of flag 34 is &#8220;Yes,&#8221; the malware communicates over HTTPS; otherwise, it communicates over HTTP.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-22-Encrypted-and-encoded-information-sent-to-C2.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-22-Encrypted-and-encoded-information-sent-to-C2.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 22 Encrypted and encoded information sent to C2.'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 22: Encrypted and encoded information sent to C2</em></p>
<p>The malware receives the encrypted and custom Base64 encoded data from the C2 server, which can be decoded using custom Base64 and decrypted using the EncryptDecrypt algorithm with the saved key in memory. At the time of analysis, the malware receives response data &#8220;1000|2000,&#8221; in which &#8220;1000&#8221; is the command to perform action and &#8220;2000&#8221; is the additional data used in performing the action which varies based on the command value.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-23-C2-Communication.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-23-C2-Communication.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 23 C2 Communication'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 23: C2 Communication</em></p>
<p>The malware performs various actions based on the command received from the C2 server. In this variant, the malware supports more than 65 commands, and a few of them are mentioned below.</p>
<p>The malware performs various actions based on the command received from the C2 server. In this variant, the malware supports more than 65 commands, and a few of them are mentioned below.</p>
<p><strong>Command: 1000 (Continue)</strong></p>
<p>Sleeps based on the value from additional data separated by &#8220;|&#8221; and sends the machine information again to C2.</p>
<p><strong>Command: 1111 (Ransomware)</strong></p>
<p>The malware retrieves the ransom note and ransomware payload bytes from additional data, which are separated by &#8220;||-<em>-|-</em>-||&#8221;. The malware drops the ransom note into the directory &#8220;C:\temp&#8221; and executes the ransomware binary.</p>
<p><strong>Command: 1065 (WebBrowserPassView)</strong></p>
<p>Along with the command, response data contains multiple binary file bytes, separated by &#8220;resourcesplit,&#8221; which are written into the following files:</p>
<ul>
<li>c:\temp\freebl3.dll</li>
<li>c:\temp\mozglue.dll</li>
<li>c:\temp\nss3.dll</li>
<li>c:\temp\softokn3.dll</li>
<li>WebBrowserPassView</li>
</ul>
<p>The malware executes the WebBrowserPassView to steal and send credentials to the C2 server and then deletes the created files.</p>
<p><strong>Command: 1108 (Launch DLL variant)</strong></p>
<p>Response data contains multiple binary file bytes for the DLL variant of DarkGate, separated by &#8220;||-<em>-|-</em>-||,&#8221; which are written and executed from the directory C:\temp using API ShellExecuteA.</p>
<ul>
<li>libcurl.dll</li>
<li>test.txt</li>
<li>GUP.exe</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Command: 1104 (Launch AHK variant)</strong></p>
<p>Response data contains multiple binary file bytes for the AutoHotKey variant of DarkGate, separated by &#8220;||-<em>-|-</em>-||,&#8221; which are written and executed from the directory C:\temp using API ShellExecuteA.</p>
<ul>
<li>script.ahk</li>
<li>text.txt</li>
<li>AutoHotKey.exe</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Command: 1097 (Launch AU3 variant)</strong></p>
<p>Response data contains multiple binary file bytes for the AutoHotKey variant of DarkGate, separated by &#8220;||-<em>-|-</em>-||,&#8221; which are written and executed from the directory C:\temp using API ShellExecuteA.</p>
<ul>
<li>script.a3x</li>
<li>Autoit3.exe</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Command: 1084 (Restart)</strong></p>
<p>Restarts the victim&#8217;s machine immediately after closing running applications using command &#8220;cmd&#46;exe &#47;&#99; shutdown -f -r -t 0&#8221;. </p>
<p><strong>Command: 1110 (Enumerate Drives)</strong></p>
<p>Enumerates system drives except CD-ROM.</p>
<p><strong>Command: 1083 (Shutdown)</strong></p>
<p>Shuts down the victim&#8217;s machine immediately after closing running applications using command &#8220;cmd&#46;exe &#47;&#99; shutdown -f -s -t 0&#8221;. </p>
<p><strong>Command: 1082 (Shutdown Display)</strong></p>
<p>The malware runs in an infinite loop to keep shutting down the victim&#8217;s display using API SendMessageA by broadcasting message &#8220;WM_SYSCOMMAND&#8221; and setting SC_MONITORPOWER with the value &#8220;2&#8221;.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-24-API-call-to-shutdown-display.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-24-API-call-to-shutdown-display.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 24 API call to shutdown display'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 24: API call to shut down display</em></p>
<p><strong>Command: 1081 (BSOD)</strong></p>
<p>The malware generates a hard error with the value &#8220;0xC0000350&#8221; using API NtRaiseHardError, which displays the BSOD (Blue Screen of Death).</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-25-Blue-Screen-of-Death.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-25-Blue-Screen-of-Death.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 25 Blue Screen of Death'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p><em>Figure 25: Blue Screen of Death</em></p>
<p><strong>Command: 1071 (FileZilla)</strong></p>
<p>The malware sends the content of the following files from &#8220;%appdata%\FileZilla&#8221; to the C2 server:</p>
<ul>
<li>recentservers.xml</li>
<li>sitemanager.xml</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Command: 1059 (Terminate Process)</strong></p>
<p>The malware terminates the process associated with the received process ID.</p>
<p>Unavailability of the PDF file in any of the popular threat intelligence sharing portals like VirusTotal and ReversingLabs at the time of writing this blog indicates its uniqueness and limited distribution.</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-26.-VT-screenshot.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-26.-VT-screenshot.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 26. VT screenshot'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p>Figure 26. VT screenshot</p>
<p>Evidence of detection by RTDMI can be seen below in the Capture ATP report for this file:</p>
<div class='avia-image-container  av-styling-     avia-align-center '  itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject"  ><div class='avia-image-container-inner'><a href="https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-27-Capture-Report-1030x531.jpg" class="avia_image" data-wpel-link="external" target="_blank" rel="external noopener noreferrer"><img class='avia_image ' src='https://d3ik27cqx8s5ub.cloudfront.net/blog/media/uploads/Figure-27-Capture-Report.jpg' alt='' title='Figure 27 Capture Report'  itemprop="thumbnailUrl"  /></a></div></div>
<p>Figure 27: Capture Report</p>
<p><strong>IOCs </strong></p>
